<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_862803008">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/862803008" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8076934-fiona-marsden" class="left imgcol" title="Fiona Marsden"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1400318079p2/8076934.jpg" alt="Fiona Marsden" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/862803008?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8076934-fiona-marsden" class="user" title="Fiona Marsden">Fiona Marsden</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth" title="Paperback 9780553132397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer862803008">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2670259265537548856">This is a fascinating story of not one but two romances. When I first read it *cough* in my teens, I was enthralled by the wicked protagonist Tracey "Devil" Belmanoir and loved the romance with Jack and Diana. But this time I found myself taking more notice of the secondary romance between Lavinia and Richard, Jack's brother.<br /><br />This an adventurous romp about Jack Carstares who takes the blame for his brother's cheating and is exiled from society. We enter the story when Richard has encountered Jack</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2670259265537548856">This is a fascinating story of not one but two romances. When I first read it *cough* in my teens, I was enthralled by the wicked protagonist Tracey "Devil" Belmanoir and loved the romance with Jack and Diana. But this time I found myself taking more notice of the secondary romance between Lavinia and Richard, Jack's brother.<br /><br />This an adventurous romp about Jack Carstares who takes the blame for his brother's cheating and is exiled from society. We enter the story when Richard has encountered Jack as a highwayman and the Earl, their father is dead. Jack is the rightful Earl and Richard is anxious he have his inheritence but is still unable to bear the thought of disgrace if he admits his guilt. He has his wife Lavinia to consider now and she is against him revealing the truth.<br /><br />The story follows Jack rescuing Diana from Devil's clutches only to fall victim to love and finding his sacrifice hard to bear. Richard is increasingly consumed by guilt, thinking of Jack in want and alone.<br /><br />All through it we have the Duke of Andover, Belmanoir, manipulating events and plotting the ravishment of Diana, though even he seems so taken with her he might consider marriage.<br /><br />This is a wonderful story with a fabulous cast of characters. Some say it isn't her best, but it is her first and quite amazing. I love how it all comes together at the end for happy endings.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2670259265537548856">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_91833066">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91833066" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1695468-brande-waldron" class="left imgcol" title="Brande Waldron"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1268069915p2/1695468.jpg" alt="Brande Waldron" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/91833066?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 28, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1695468-brande-waldron" class="user" title="Brande Waldron">Brande Waldron</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth" title="Paperback 9780553132397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer91833066">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10142564461264629859">With a special thanks to Danielle @ Sourcebooks I was introduced to Georgette Heyer and the first novel in a four-part series including These Old Shades, Devil’s Cub, and An Infamous Army, The Black Moth. This is also her first novel and in the true spirit of what first opened my eyes to Jane Austin, I have found a new author to follow. An eccentric setting in this Regency Historical, with all the perfect, prim and proper but doesn't fail to be witty and cunning with sharp dialog that kept me en</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10142564461264629859">With a special thanks to Danielle @ Sourcebooks I was introduced to Georgette Heyer and the first novel in a four-part series including These Old Shades, Devil’s Cub, and An Infamous Army, The Black Moth. This is also her first novel and in the true spirit of what first opened my eyes to Jane Austin, I have found a new author to follow. An eccentric setting in this Regency Historical, with all the perfect, prim and proper but doesn't fail to be witty and cunning with sharp dialog that kept me entertained throughout.This is the story of Jack Carstares the Earl of Wyncham who was forced to leave because he was wrongly accused of cheating at cards. A pretty noble thing if you ask me since he was covering for his brother, but now he is a Highway man, robbing others to survive and actually ends up holding up his brother Richard in the process unbeknownst to his brother. Duke of Andover, Tracy "Devil" Belmanoir, in his attempt to "rescue" the young and very beautiful socialite Diana Beauleigh in order to make her or rather fool her into being his next conquest. But his plan is thwarted as The Black Moth attempts to kidnap her, as gallant Jack actually ends up saving her. Enamored and grateful to Jack, Diana takes him home with her and her aunt for him to recover from his wounds, which only forces the two to spends more time together. As their feelings start to grow for each other Jack realized that this can never be, she is a lady and he is a criminal. It would be scandalous. The Duke or "Devil" is known for all his scandalous affairs with a bevy of women and is able to work his charms on Diana as well or so he believes. Poor Diana is confused as ever with the affections and attentions of both men, and not quite sure of the identity of either. A game they are playing with her turns into something a little more real as Diana and Jack start to develop feelings for each other. Oh what a tangled web we weave. So much high society drama I was impressed to find out that Georgette Hayer wrote this at such a young age and even more impressed with the fact that it was so much better than I had imagined. And just like Jane Austin there is such an interesting side character's that truly makes this a cohesive piece or romance, honor, and vengeance. Throughout the story I felt like I was on a mission to find out who exactly The Black Moth was.... and I can't tell you! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10142564461264629859">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/352670381?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 21, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3690472-thom-swennes" class="user" title="Thom Swennes">Thom Swennes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth" title="Paperback 9780553132397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer352670381">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1422392634186828817">The Black Moth is the first novel of the highly prolific romance author Georgette Heyer and rivals the works of her 19th Century colleagues. Written in 1921, The Black Moth is the story of the lives and follies of England’s 18th Century aristocracy. Although the story is well written, I ‘m not very impressed with its contents. I have no doubt that this class is correctly portrayed, their lives don’t make for particularly interesting reading. The way they waste their lives on trifles, pleasures a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1422392634186828817">The Black Moth is the first novel of the highly prolific romance author Georgette Heyer and rivals the works of her 19th Century colleagues. Written in 1921, The Black Moth is the story of the lives and follies of England’s 18th Century aristocracy. Although the story is well written, I ‘m not very impressed with its contents. I have no doubt that this class is correctly portrayed, their lives don’t make for particularly interesting reading. The way they waste their lives on trifles, pleasures and gambling isn’t appealing or fascinating enough to build a book around. Georgette Heyer was one of the last of her age and soon replaced with writers with a different style. One of these later romance writers was Kathleen E. Woodiweiss (The Flame and the Fire and Shanna). These later writers wrote around more explicit sexual attractions, confrontations and liaisons; increasing the story interest drastically. This book and most of the stories written in this style aren’t really my cup of tea. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1422392634186828817">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_82234435">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82234435" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/883725-julianna" class="left imgcol" title="Julianna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442976820p2/883725.jpg" alt="Julianna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/82234435?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 02, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/883725-julianna" class="user" title="Julianna">Julianna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/2899966-the-black-moth" title="Paperback 9780373773398" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of "Classic" Romance</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=historical-romance" class="actionLinkLite">historical-romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/883725-julianna?shelf=read-2010" class="actionLinkLite">read-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer82234435">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10149568399760772190">Reviewed for <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.thcreviews.com/">THC Reviews</a><br />Until picking up <em>The Black Moth</em>, I had never read a “classical” romance, and I have to say that it was a rather different sort of reading experience that was more challenging than the typical modern romance. It is written in what I would call a literary style with vernacular that is more authentic to the time period in which it is set. It was a little difficult to keep track of all the characters, because there were so many and each one went by several different names (fi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10149568399760772190">Reviewed for <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.thcreviews.com/">THC Reviews</a><br />Until picking up <em>The Black Moth</em>, I had never read a “classical” romance, and I have to say that it was a rather different sort of reading experience that was more challenging than the typical modern romance. It is written in what I would call a literary style with vernacular that is more authentic to the time period in which it is set. It was a little difficult to keep track of all the characters, because there were so many and each one went by several different names (first, last, nickname, alias, title). It was all somewhat confusing, but I think I managed to keep up fairly well. Also, the romance itself is very low-key with nothing beyond a few chaste kisses and embraces occurring. The palette of characters in <em>The Black Moth</em> was more of an ensemble cast with the supposed main hero and heroine only present in approximately fifty percent of the scenes and few of those were in each other's company. All of this made the book feel a bit more like historical fiction than historical romance to me, although I have to admit that the climax was pretty romantic with the hero swooping in to save the heroine from the dastardly villain so they can live happily-ever-after.:-)<br /><br />Jack was a very noble hero, the heir to an earldom, who sacrificed himself for his brother in the name of love. Ever since he took the blame for cheating at a game of cards, he has been living in virtual exile, and has taken up the profession of highwayman, albeit in the style of Robin Hood. He always gives what he takes to the poor, and he refuses to rob ladies or the elderly. On the rare occasions that he makes that mistake, he apologizes profusely which was rather amusing. When he's not playing the roguish thief, he is a dandy who always tries to be at the height of fashion. Jack is the classic dashing, debonair hero, full of charm and lighthearted spirit in spite of his lot in life. When he saves the lovely Diana, they fall madly in love, although the development of their relationship is pretty much left to the imagination. <br /><br />Diana was a sweet girl who had unfortunately caught the unwanted attentions of the Duke of Andover, a notorious libertine. When the villainous Duke kidnaps Diana in an attempt to force her to wed him, she is rescued by Jack. She falls for her savior while he is recovering from his wounds at her home, and is terribly distraught when he is unable to return her affections and leaves. Diana was in even less scenes than Jack, so I didn't really feel like I got to know her well, but I will say that I admired her spunk and determination, as well as her ability to verbally spar with the Duke when he attempted to take her a second time.<br /><br />As I mentioned earlier, <em>The Black Moth</em> boasts a very large cast of characters, some of whom are equally as important as Jack and Diana. Jack's brother, Richard and his wife, Lavinia would be at the top of that list, experiencing their own romantic ups and downs. Richard had allowed Jack to take the blame for his actions, because he loved Lavinia so much he couldn't bear the thought of loosing her if the truth came out. He has lived with the guilt ever since, which has taken it's toll on him. At first his actions seemed selfish, leaving me unsure as to whether I liked him or not. He also had a tendency to be a doormat for his wife which wasn't particularly endearing either, but I was pleased to see him grow and change throughout the story. Lavinia was a difficult character to like, because she always seemed so spoiled and shrewish. In fact, she and all of her brothers were pretty much portrayed as hedonists with little or no self-control. Although I don't think I'll ever truly understand why all the men in her life were so taken with her, I did at least gain a small measure of respect for her by the end when she finally realized what a good life and husband she had. The other top-tier character would be Lavinia's brother, Tracy, the Duke of Andover, who became obsessed with Diana. In my opinion, he was a dangerous miscreant to have kidnapped and tried to force an innocent young woman who obviously didn't want him into marriage. I'm usually used to seeing the villain get a more sound comeuppance than Tracy did, so the way things ended with him were somewhat unsatisfying. However, I suppose that in a historical sense, sending a duke to jail is not something that would typically have happened. I also think the author was trying to show that he had perhaps learned from the experience and turned over a new leaf, although I'm not so sure I believe that. Although <em>The Black Moth</em> is not officially considered part of a series, it is my understanding that the Duke does return in <em>These Old Shades</em>.<br /><br /><em>The Black Moth</em> was definitely a change of pace from my usual romance reading. Although the story contains large swaths of dialog, punctuated by shorter passages of prose, I felt that Ms. Heyer gave the reader a good feel of what it was like to live in the Georgian era by imparting many little details of fashion and society. In fact, I learned a few new and surprising things. I might point out here as well, that a number of individuals and a few websites have this book mislabeled as Regency romance, and although the exact year is never given in the text, the historical details clearly place it in the Georgian time frame. I felt that the pacing of the book was rather up and down with some parts moving pretty slowly, leaving my mind wandering, and others being engaging and fast-paced. It took me a while to get used to how different this book was than the romances I typically read, but it ended up being an agreeable experience. Based on the ratings I've seen on various book-related sites, I'd say that <em>The Black Moth</em> doesn't appear to be a fan favorite, but it was good enough to make me want to pick up another Georgette Heyer book in the future. If one takes into account that Ms. Heyer wrote the book when she was only seventeen to entertain her sick brother, I'd say she did quite well for one so young.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10149568399760772190">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/11308543-the-black-moth" title="Nook 2940012308375" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer443614264">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9510738630292743664">I started this book as something lighter to read as a break between Dunnett's epic series. It was just that. An easy read, a light hearted melodrama lacking only those tongue-in-cheek asides to the audience that let you know everything is in fun. And indeed it is. <br /><br />Richard Carstares cannot live with his conscience over what he did on that night seven years ago, causing his beloved big brother John to abandon his Earldom, flee to Europe, returning after a time to live and roam as a highwayman, but</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9510738630292743664">I started this book as something lighter to read as a break between Dunnett's epic series. It was just that. An easy read, a light hearted melodrama lacking only those tongue-in-cheek asides to the audience that let you know everything is in fun. And indeed it is. <br /><br />Richard Carstares cannot live with his conscience over what he did on that night seven years ago, causing his beloved big brother John to abandon his Earldom, flee to Europe, returning after a time to live and roam as a highwayman, but attacking only the wealthy and giving to the needy, much like Robin Hood. Richard's wife, Lavinia, is annoying, to me the reader, but not to Richard, who bears her shenanigans and moods with true love and affection. After all, Lavinia was part of problem that long ago night, loved by both brothers. Then of course there is the beautiful Diana -with that name how could she be otherwise? - who captures the eye of John as well as the villain. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ In true melodrama manner, must not the hero rescue the maiden? Not once but twice? Must not his name be cleared? Must not the villain be punished and marched off to prison? Or not. Maybe we could all just discuss this over dinner.... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I understand this was Heyer's first novel, written at a very young age to entertain a younger sibling who was ill. I don't know if it was intended as a melodrama but certainly strikes me as such. It was a fun read, but not something I would read again. She appears to have a large following for her many books, so we may assume her style matures. Perhaps I'll give a later book a try. <!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9510738630292743664">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_67035289">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67035289" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/263316-shawna" class="left imgcol" title="Shawna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1186587603p2/263316.jpg" alt="Shawna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/67035289?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 08, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/263316-shawna" class="user" title="Shawna">Shawna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth" title="Paperback 9780553132397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/263316-shawna?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer67035289'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer67035289">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18405304148259317529">Quite the melodramatic romance. The villain even wears black and he would be twirling his mustache if he had one (though I sometimes enjoyed his character). The basic plot is that the hero takes the blame for his cheating little brother and goes into self-imposed exile. The hero returns, with several identities. He is now both a highway man and a dandy. I have never read so much about men preening, powdering, and worrying about their outfit then in this book but I suppose that more has to do wit</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18405304148259317529">Quite the melodramatic romance. The villain even wears black and he would be twirling his mustache if he had one (though I sometimes enjoyed his character). The basic plot is that the hero takes the blame for his cheating little brother and goes into self-imposed exile. The hero returns, with several identities. He is now both a highway man and a dandy. I have never read so much about men preening, powdering, and worrying about their outfit then in this book but I suppose that more has to do with the time period. Hero saves lady fair from the villain while he is trying to kidnap her from her carriage. She beautifully struggles while two men hold her back during the sword fight. And of course lady fair and hero fall in love while he recovers from his injuries from saving her.<br /><br />I disliked the ending. Mr. Villain (The Black Moth) successfully captures the lady fair and plans marry (and *cough* you know) against her will. There is a huge confrontation with the hero and several other characters. Hero wins, villain shrugs and invites them all to dinner because it turns out he is an old friend. No punishment or care.<br /><br />My favorite characters were a married couple (O’Hares? Can they be considered favorites if I cannot remember their names?) who were extremely devoted and adorable.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18405304148259317529">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/3555151-pip" class="user" title="Pip">Pip</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10092591854751911866">I liked that the worst people in this book are more or less the main characters! I prefer characters who are acknowledged as terrible, but are still human, to ones who are held up as supposedly perfect paragons (and Jack does not impress me with how quickly he shrugs off the first attempted abduction/rape of Diana with "Oh I don't know. I dare say we are none of us exactly saints." &lt;-- Actual quote. WHAT. Even his friend is taken aback. This is not romantic hero material in my book).</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_628119984">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/628119984" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6874915-trudy-brasure" class="left imgcol" title="Trudy Brasure"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442609699p2/6874915.jpg" alt="Trudy Brasure" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/628119984?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 29, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6874915-trudy-brasure" class="user" title="Trudy Brasure">Trudy Brasure</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth" title="Paperback 9780553132397" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer628119984">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10738518683299498855">I would never have guessed that this was Heyer's first novel. It was better written than "Powder and Patch." Her character descriptions are always enjoyable and her renown historical detail is already firmly in place. This is a Georgian Era piece, and not her traditional Regency. I think this is the only title character that isn't really the hero of the story. The Black Moth instead refers to the hero's nemesis. Strange title choice. <br />My strongest complaint is that although she takes her time to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10738518683299498855">I would never have guessed that this was Heyer's first novel. It was better written than "Powder and Patch." Her character descriptions are always enjoyable and her renown historical detail is already firmly in place. This is a Georgian Era piece, and not her traditional Regency. I think this is the only title character that isn't really the hero of the story. The Black Moth instead refers to the hero's nemesis. Strange title choice. <br />My strongest complaint is that although she takes her time to introduce the many characters of the story, she rushes through the development of the hero's affection for his lady love. Instead of describing multiple encounters between the couple to show their growing attachment, we are given a brief domestic scene and then a bit of narrative actually telling us that they fell in love. It's hard to rate a Heyer so low, I generally enjoy all her books. But there are some worth reading over again. I don't think this is one of them.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10738518683299498855">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//&lt;![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eBZQYfnZhD31pIjCq5/0xOMrHyXH5vLmqdRU8Hcfz8Mdwm+yCwuQx3l8/HnV/hJ2qtzcPI47tUzD3VFiu4o5hg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QhAoCeMfjRwl+8UT8UHDpgyixoQpwKASf1yIMJPcV0wnxBfaEc2Z5qkjsaiPICUURVUFnWAd57gVVY2iX0mhCQ==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>

Before it is too late.&amp;quot; Those were the half-naked stranger\'s only words as he opened his eyes, looked into her soul, then lost consciousness in the shallow tidal pool. Annaleah Fairchilde, heiress and noblewoman, soon learned the identity of the man she saved: Emory Althorpe, adventurer, accused traitor, and Napoleon\'s spy. Even as she bound the dangerous scoundrel\'s wounds, she fell deeper under the spell of a man handsome as the devil, sent to her by fate.He remembered nothing. Only the need to escape. Emory Althorpe was wanted for treason, yet his only hope was a shadowy memory hovering just beyond his grasp, and beautiful Anna, who aroused unexpected passions. He had no choice but to abduct her, needing her to gain access to London\'s ballrooms, where he could prove his innocence. Together they raced into the face of danger, determined to find the key to his past and live--or die--together, as destiny decreed....&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"16728697642430571064\"     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/18067.Georgette_Heyer">About Georgette Heyer</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/18067.Georgette_Heyer"><img alt="18067" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1336748892p2/18067.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer18392327321043053684">Georgette Heyer was a prolific historical romance and detective fiction novelist. Her writing career began in 1921, when she turned a story for her younger brother into the novel <a title=" The Black Moth" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311322._The_Black_Moth"> The Black Moth</a>.<br /><br />In 1925 she married George Ronald Rougier, a mining engineer, and he often provided basic plot outlines for her thrillers. Beginning in 1932, Heyer released one romance novel and one thriller each year.<br /><br />Hey</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18392327321043053684">Georgette Heyer was a prolific historical romance and detective fiction novelist. Her writing career began in 1921, when she turned a story for her younger brother into the novel <a title=" The Black Moth" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311322._The_Black_Moth"> The Black Moth</a>.<br /><br />In 1925 she married George Ronald Rougier, a mining engineer, and he often provided basic plot outlines for her thrillers. Beginning in 1932, Heyer released one romance novel and one thriller each year.<br /><br />Heyer was an intensely private person who remained a best selling author all her life without the aid of publicity. She made no appearances, never gave an interview and only answered fan letters herself if they made an interesting historical point. She sometimes wrote under the pseudonym Stella Martin.<br /><br />Her Regencies were inspired by <a title=" Jane Austen" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1265._Jane_Austen"> Jane Austen</a>, but unlike Austen, who wrote about and for the times in which she lived, Heyer was forced to include copious information about the period so that her readers would understand the setting. While some critics thought her novels were too detailed, others considered the level of detail to be Heyer's greatest asset.<br /><br />Heyer remains a popular and much-loved author, known for essentially establishing the historical romance genre and its subgenre Regency romance.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18392327321043053684">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/18067.Georgette_Heyer" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Georgette Heyer...</a>
